Foch's Memoirs of the European War Continued 226

— Translated by Gu Angruo

After being entrusted by the governments of the two Entente countries, I finalized my battle plan based on the instructions I presented to the French Prime Minister two days prior, restating it as follows:

(1) The French and British armies must be closely connected to protect Amiens.

(2) To this end, the troops already engaged in battle must hold their positions.

(3) Under their protection, the French reinforcements, after disembarking, would first reinforce the British Fifth Army and second assemble and await orders.

The plans for the British army to fight alone to save the English Channel and for the French army to fight alone to save Paris were canceled. We now decided that the French and British armies would fight together to save Amiens, which was the point of intersection of the two armies.

I further drafted detailed implementation rules. In times of urgency, I explained the plan in detail to the relevant senior officers. Then, at 4:00 PM, I went with General Gough, commander of the British Fifth Army, to Dury. I instructed him to remain at the front line and strictly order his troops to hold their positions and await the arrival of the French army, starting partial adjustments from the south.

I also met General Foye's Chief of Staff, General Buserlemi, at Dury, and gave him the same instructions: he must hold Amiens.

I asked Major Malang, the liaison officer of the French headquarters, to report these military orders to General Petain. I also notified General Depenne, commander of the First Army, by telephone, instructing him on how to proceed.

This army was being deployed towards Montdidier, its position being particularly important because the

Page 20

reconnection of the French and British lines depended entirely on it. I connected the telephone from Dury to Menard and spoke directly with General Depenne, explaining that his mission was to have him send all his troops to the front line to replace the British 18th Corps. The replaced troops were to be transferred to General Gough as reserves. He must connect with the British troops on the left, towards Rocheleau, and hold the line.

On the same day, the 26th, I notified all the commanders on the front line of my plan. In the evening, General Petain canceled the instructions of March 24th and ordered General Foye to protect Amiens and maintain contact with the troops of General Haig. He further ordered the transfer of ten infantry divisions and four artillery regiments from other corps to reinforce the reserves.

After spending one night in Paris, I set out for the front line again on the morning of the 27th, accompanied by General Weygand and Colonel Didier. Before setting out, I wrote a secret letter to General Petain, urging him to proceed according to the plan and asking him to explain its necessity to the French army. General Petain and his subordinates were able to cooperate effectively and meet the enemy without further explanation.

I first went to the headquarters of General Humbel, commander of the Third Corps, in Clermont, where I met General Foye. I reiterated my instructions, which were to hold the line and not replace any troops until the first success was achieved.

At 12:30 PM, I went to Dury to see General Gough regarding the connection between the right wing of the British army and the left wing of the French army. This connection was not very stable due to the hasty retreat of several troops of the British 18th Corps. I explained the new plan to General Mossberg, commander of the left wing of the French First Army, and asked General Gough to personally confirm whether the British 18th Corps had received my instructions and was proceeding accordingly.

I then visited General Byng, commander of the British Third Army, in Beauquesne. He explained that the situation on his side was not serious. He expressed his full support for my battle plan and was willing to do his best to ensure its success.

Because of the lack of a general reserve force, it was not possible to restore the line from the outset. The first step that needed to be implemented urgently was to quickly weld together the various sections of the line that had been broken through and stretched by the German army. The most important thing was that all retreating soldiers or arriving reinforcements, regardless of their nationality, must be of one mind and work together. Because any plans for troop adjustments had been temporarily put on hold, there would be no hope of defeating the enemy in the next few days if this could not be achieved. (To be continued)
